<div id="review_2561414076" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2561414076" />
    <a title="Girish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5924440-girish"><img alt="Girish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591866419p2/5924440.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2561414076?book_show_action=true">Oct 26, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Girish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Girish" href="/user/show/5924440-girish">Girish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2561414076" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12590684302412661825"><i>"If I were not Napolean, I would want to be the citizen Cosimo Piovasco di Rondò" </i> exclaims Napolean Bonaparte after meeting our Baron in the trees. <br/><br/>The extrordinary tale of Cosimo Piovasco di Rondò, the baron who in adoloscent rebellion, moves into the trees and never comes down is an exercise in metaphors. 12 year old Cosimo challenges the authority of his father to make him eat snails and goes up the tree. Everyone assumes he will come back, but with the stubborness of idea, Cosimo soon start</span>
  <span id="freeText12590684302412661825" style="display:none"><i>"If I were not Napolean, I would want to be the citizen Cosimo Piovasco di Rondò" </i> exclaims Napolean Bonaparte after meeting our Baron in the trees. <br /><br />The extrordinary tale of Cosimo Piovasco di Rondò, the baron who in adoloscent rebellion, moves into the trees and never comes down is an exercise in metaphors. 12 year old Cosimo challenges the authority of his father to make him eat snails and goes up the tree. Everyone assumes he will come back, but with the stubborness of idea, Cosimo soon starts adapting to his new truth and goes on to become a legend. <br /><br />Though the response seems exaggerated (and ridiculous), don't we consider any rebellion whose ideas seem new to be silly? From his new position, he learns to exercise his free will to help people - be it gathering people to prevent forest fires, stopping pirates, reading to a brigand before his execution and later becoming an military emissary. He goes on to establish the secret society of free masons of Italy <br /><br />Calvino keeps reminding us he is still the young boy who refused to have snail. He falls in love with Viola and get's his heart broken. He tends to his ailing mother without giving up on his principle. He suffers pangs of jealousy and loneliness. In effect, a rebel just in the eyes of the society, but every bit human.<br /><br /><i>"We'll make an army in the trees and bring the earth and the people on it to their senses"</i><br /><br />The metaphor for independence is understated. When he meets a spanish tribe banished to live in the trees, the tribal head exclaims awe at his choice vs resignation of his people. The world has very few individuals of today, who act dedicated to an idea than conform to the norm. He ends up doing much more for the people, the more distant in physical space he gets away from them. Like the futility of war.<br /><br />The book has some hilarious episodes like the brigand who falls in loves with books. It does slow down considerably after the 75% stage. The narrator, his younger brother, is impartial and candidly tells us there are many versions of each episode and he isn't sure which is the truth.(this alone, in an 1950s book is quite post modernist!) But an almost fable from this author who meditates on love and it's pain caught be off guard.<br /><br />An easier Calvino, but not his most profound.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12590684302412661825" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3405201303" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3405201303" />
    <a title="Uhtred" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/109743598-uhtred"><img alt="Uhtred" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1581262401p2/109743598.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3405201303?book_show_action=true">Jun 22, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Uhtred" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Uhtred" href="/user/show/109743598-uhtred">Uhtred</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788804370857" href="/book/show/820645.Il_barone_rampante">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3405201303" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9420659320165815779">I reread after many years "The Baron in the trees", the second novel of the trilogy "Our ancestors", after having just reread the first, "The Cloven Viscount", and after this I will read the third, The Nonexistent Knight. I have a good memory of when I first read them as a teenager and I am curious to see how I will find them now, as an adult.<br/>The plot is set in the eighteenth century and is narrated in first person by Biagio, the protagonist's brother, that is Cosimo Piovasco di Rondò (without a</span>
  <span id="freeText9420659320165815779" style="display:none">I reread after many years "The Baron in the trees", the second novel of the trilogy "Our ancestors", after having just reread the first, "The Cloven Viscount", and after this I will read the third, The Nonexistent Knight. I have a good memory of when I first read them as a teenager and I am curious to see how I will find them now, as an adult.<br />The plot is set in the eighteenth century and is narrated in first person by Biagio, the protagonist's brother, that is Cosimo Piovasco di Rondò (without a name like what Baron would it be?). Cosimo is the son of a noble Ligurian family and when he is twelve years old, after a quarrel with his parents on a matter related to a plate of snails, he climbs on a tree in the home garden and never goes down again. Cosimo begins the thing like a whim, but then makes it a true lifestyle: being always in the trees he moves through woods and forests and gradually builds his complete existence on the trees. Cosimo meets Viola, a young girl he falls in love with, then finds a faithful friend in the dog Ottimo Massimo, and becomes very popular with the inhabitants of his lands. His is a path of growth in all senses: he knows the children of his country, becomes a friend of the outlaw Gian de Brughi, to whom he even teaches the beauty of reading, he studies philosophy, even becomes a pen pal of Voltaire, he organizes the inhabitants of his lands in groups against the fires in the woods, in short, he is very busy. The story with Viola, however, takes an unpleasant turn and due to a misunderstanding, the two move away and Viola will then marry another. Meanwhile, we have arrived at the time of the French Revolution and Cosimo also meets Napoleon, who however leaves him rather disappointed, as he had another idea of Napoleon. Cosimo's story ends with a beautiful twist in full Calvinian fairytale style: Cosimo, now grown old and tired, keeps his promise until the end and does not go ashore, but at the passage of a hot air balloon, he clings to a dangling rope and disappears on the horizon, leaving us as if we saw it disappear.<br />The story is very original: Cosimo's choice is not an escape from the world and from the people: his choice is the will of a man who wants to fully follow a promise he made to himself, as if without this promise you couldn't tell who Cosimo really is. It is a bit twisted but it is as if Cosimo thought that to live fully and be of real help to others the only way for him was to be separated from the others. It is true that Cosimo necessarily looks down on everyone, but he does it much less than certain misanthropes living on earth ...<br />As a teenager I had read it as a children's book, although they told me that it could be read on several levels, but in school age you can see above all the "light" part of the plot, which is however beautiful. As an adult I find these sensations again, but for sure with many other things: Calvino’s paradoxical characters and his improbable stories are not parodies, instead, they can be metaphors. Cosimo's choice seems far-fetched, but his constancy, his stubbornness, his coherence are the seal of those who believe in a cause to the end, so much so as to dedicate his whole life to it. You may not agree with them, but the consistency of certain people certainly impresses you. Cosimo does not accept compromises, he is faithful to himself and his promise, without regards to how uncomfortable it is. Cosimo climbs the tree on a whim as a child, but does not remain a child on the tree: over the branches his growth continues, solitary but not a voluntary hermit. He lives the passing of the seasons, reads many books, recognizes the sense of power when people called him Mister Baron, knows love and also sex, becomes an adult and then becomes old, with always that small distance from the earth (a jump would be enough ) which is like an impassable wall. We know Cosimo as a twelve year old and then we see him as an old man, hunchbacked and wrinkled, with his hairy coat, consistent up to the impossible. Impossible for us, not for Cosimo, and not for Calvino's ideal, who presents us with the refusal of compromise as the only way to defend the truest part of us, up to grab that hook in the middle of the sky and disappear on the horizon . Definitely 6 stars.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9420659320165815779" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3182394129" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3182394129" />
    <a title="sologdin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5610133-sologdin"><img alt="sologdin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307928658p2/5610133.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3182394129?book_show_action=true">Feb 08, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="sologdin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="sologdin" href="/user/show/5610133-sologdin">sologdin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5610133-sologdin?shelf=of-best-sentence-and-moost-solaas">of-best-sentence-and-moost-solaas</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3182394129" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16632618296968777961">Calvino reports, in one of the <i>Hermit in Paris</i> essays, that he wrote this novel in response to leaving the communist party, after the 1956 Soviet invasion of Hungary. I'm nevertheless not getting a god-that-failed critique vibe here. The novel's setting involves the destruction of the French Revolution by reactionaries (so there's that concordance with 1956)--and the protagonist is a proponent of the revolution--he is likely in the revolution's leftwing, envisioning a radical arborocracy that gr</span>
  <span id="freeText16632618296968777961" style="display:none">Calvino reports, in one of the <i>Hermit in Paris</i> essays, that he wrote this novel in response to leaving the communist party, after the 1956 Soviet invasion of Hungary. I'm nevertheless not getting a god-that-failed critique vibe here. The novel's setting involves the destruction of the French Revolution by reactionaries (so there's that concordance with 1956)--and the protagonist is a proponent of the revolution--he is likely in the revolution's leftwing, envisioning a radical arborocracy that grants rights even to animals and plants. He's accordingly up there with Tom Bombadil, which places him in excellent company. Am not detecting a refutation of his arborescent ideal. That said, "We live in a country where causes are always seen but never effects."<br /><br />The text is almost an inverted companion of Murakami's <i>Wind Up Bird Chronicle</i>, which features a guy who goes into a hole in the ground in order to work out his problem; he does not write a chthonian manifesto--unlike the arboreal baron--identifying the chthonic with unrestrained and unreflective id--very much the metaphor here.<br /><br />Otherwise engaging. An oddity insofar as protagonist is seemingly dominated by an inconsistently available and oscillating Ligeia figure whom he nevertheless abandons to remain in the trees.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16632618296968777961" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText10550033454828656740" style="display:none">When i started reading this book, i figured, alright, its just a sweet little story that happens to be famous. I should have read it a long time ago when i was a kid. But as it went forward with kuzimo growing up and his ability to learn about love, I felt like Im reading about my own life. Those feelings of rushing love you get when you are with the one person you care about the most, is not something you can pass from easily. Also, the book explains everything with a super simplified, yet rich literature, in a way that made me finish it in a few hours. I loved reading it. It stimulated so many parts of my brain related to nostalgia, ambitions and dreams and touched my heart in so many places that cant be described thru one little note :)<br /></span>

<div id="review_3207398083" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3207398083" />
    <a title="Ali Karimnejad" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/93895577-ali-karimnejad"><img alt="Ali Karimnejad" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591439739p2/93895577.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3207398083?book_show_action=true">Feb 27, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ali Karimnejad" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ali Karimnejad" href="/user/show/93895577-ali-karimnejad">Ali Karimnejad</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789643510176" href="/book/show/1032098._">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3207398083" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13356028799951535172">"The Baron in the Trees" not only couldn't satisfy me as an adult guy, but also for the teenagers I doubt it would be an appropriate book. not because our main character rebels and escapes from home, but because Cosimo, decides to climb up the trees and be there for the rest of his life only because he decided to do so. <br/><br/>I think something here has been really messed up and it is "what makes Cosimo a great man?" what will be remembered about him? In the way the book narrates the story, all the foc</span>
  <span id="freeText13356028799951535172" style="display:none">"The Baron in the Trees" not only couldn't satisfy me as an adult guy, but also for the teenagers I doubt it would be an appropriate book. not because our main character rebels and escapes from home, but because Cosimo, decides to climb up the trees and be there for the rest of his life only because he decided to do so. <br /><br />I think something here has been really messed up and it is "what makes Cosimo a great man?" what will be remembered about him? In the way the book narrates the story, all the focus is on being up the trees. Cosimo takes an oath to never put his foot on the ground and he pays hefty prices for that. but he never breaks his oath. Is it something we should admire?! Should we read this story for our children? I don't think so. Cosimo is the hero because of other characteristics he have and they never get fair attention. instead, it is keeping his word that is mentioned over and over. There is no doubt that keeping your word is praiseworthy but one should never forget to step back from his wrong decisions, or unnecessary actions that hinders his life. Couldn't Cosimo be a great man if he put his foot on the ground? In this way, I found Cosimo a stubborn guy. although it is wrapped in beautiful words. <br /><br />we may argue that ground is a symbol of all restrictions and the trees are a symbol of freedom. and all the efforts Cosimo puts in order to avoid the ground is because he wants to be free. but unfortunately this is not the case as we never read about supernatural characteristics of the trees or any metaphors about them. all said about trees are just their names: Walnut, Oak, Fig, Cherry, etc. <br /><br />to conclude, While "The Baron on the Trees" has a novel idea and very good material, it fails to make a good story for younger ages and for myself, it lacks many things. but still I liked the way it ended.<br /><br />پا فشاری بیش از حد روی یک حرف، فرقی با ایمان تزلزل ناپذیر اهالی کلیسا نداره. نمی فهمم چطور نویسنده روی درخت بودن کازیمو به هر قیمتی رو ستایش می کنه اما به راهب ها می توپه. انسانی که دنده عقب نداشته باشه از آدمیت دور میشه. به نظرم کتاب نتونست پتانسیل بالایی رو که داشت بالفعل کنه و ایده کتاب تلف شد.<br />با این حال یک جمله ای داشت که یادگاری از این کتاب باسم می مونه: <br />«بسیار دیده شده است که مردمانی که برای آرمانی نه چندان روشن و مشخص مبارزه می کنند مجبورند برای جبران گنگی و سستی انگیزه هایشان ظاهری بسیار جدی به خود بدهند.»</span>
  <a data-text-id="13356028799951535172" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_3207398083'><a id="like_count_review_3207398083" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/3207398083?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">7 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F9804.The_Baron_in_the_Trees">Like</a></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_150120553" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/150120553" />
    <a title="Mike" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5026888-mike"><img alt="Mike" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1298511485p2/5026888.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/150120553?book_show_action=true">Feb 23, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike" href="/user/show/5026888-mike">Mike</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer150120553" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18126893085713433895">When you read multiple works by an author, you start to identify characteristics and themes that unify the oeuvre and allow for adjectives like "Kafkaesque" and "Joycean." <i>The Baron in the Trees</i> is the fourth novel I've read by Italo Calvino, who I consider one of my favorite authors. My experience with Calvino has been somewhat odd, however, because the order in which I've read these four books is almost exactly the opposite order in which he wrote them. I have no doubt that an elderly Calvino </span>
  <span id="freeText18126893085713433895" style="display:none">When you read multiple works by an author, you start to identify characteristics and themes that unify the oeuvre and allow for adjectives like "Kafkaesque" and "Joycean." <i>The Baron in the Trees</i> is the fourth novel I've read by Italo Calvino, who I consider one of my favorite authors. My experience with Calvino has been somewhat odd, however, because the order in which I've read these four books is almost exactly the opposite order in which he wrote them. I have no doubt that an elderly Calvino would enjoy that chronological discrepancy and have a few creative ideas to share about it.<br /><br />Calvino was a prominent member of the Oulipo—a group of authors, mainly French and Italian, famous for their formal experimentation with narrative. Raymond Queneau's <i>Exercises in Style</i>, for example, consists of 99 versions of the same basic story, each in a unique style. I've always wanted to read it, but haven't been able to find it anywhere. <i>[Update: Found this at Myopic Books in Chicago!]</i> Another I've wanted to try is Georges Perec's <i>A Void</i>, written entirely without the letter "e." The story is about a group of friends missing and searching for one of their companions... kinda like, you know, the alphabet of the story itself. They get dangerously close to finding him/it, but never can, given Perec's self-imposed linguistic rule. Neat, right?<br /><br />Well, up until now I've only read works by Calvino written after he joined the Oulipo. <i>The Baron in the Trees</i>, however, was written a good decade before he was invited to the group. Thus, rather than seeing Calvino develop his own themes/characteristics/etc. and then see him begin to play with them formally as a member of the Oulipo, my experience of Calvino is the reverse: the themes/characteristics/etc. that whisper amongst playful stylistic innovation shake off their formal constraints to prominently emerge in a more traditional narrative.<br /><br /><i>The Baron in the Trees</i> is about a boy named Cosimo who refuses to eat his dinner one night (roasted snails—I agree dude, F that) and protests by climbing into the trees and vowing to never return to the ground. The story, narrated by his younger brother, is pretty episodic, with each chapter a different little tale about Cosimo: the time he fought off pirates, the time he helped prevent a forest fire, the time he helped hide a thief, etc. It's a classic form of storytelling, dating back to Homer's <i>Odyssey</i> really. I prefer the Oulipo Calvino who experiments and plays around with these traditional storytelling aspects, but this earlier, more traditional Calvino was nice too.<br /><br /><i>The Baron in the Trees</i> is very whimsical. Some stories about Cosimo are more believable than others, and actually, the varying degrees of credibility are quite interesting. As Cosimo stays longer and longer in the trees, more and more people learn about him and, inevitably, the rumors and stories about him get more and more exaggerated. Cosimo's younger brother occasionally says things like, "I saw such and such myself, and I know that..." or, "It has been said that my brother... I'm not certain but..." etc. Cosimo goes from simply interacting with his family and neighbors in the opening chapters, to meeting a group of people also living in the trees (only out of exile, rather than choice), to corresponding with Rousseau and Voltaire by letter, to conversing with Napoleon! It's fun and clever and, as I said before, whimsical.<br /><br />Of course there is plenty to analyze sociologically/philosophically. A story about a boy living in the trees, removed from yet still interacting with human society will inevitably have its observations of and comments on both parties. Some of the more interesting themes were loneliness, and the questions of how people unite to solve problems greater than themselves, and why these groups inevitably falter and become detached again. Cosimo does manage to have affairs with women and even falls deeply in love with one. The episodes with Viola, his true love, were some of my favorites, as they reveal how complicated love can be: jealousy, commitment, devotion, self and other, violence, anger, misunderstandings...<br /><br />To be honest, towards the end I did find myself just wanting to finish it so I could move on to a new book. However, this is more a result of my impatience than any failure by Calvino. If asked for a recommendation, I would not choose The Baron in the Trees for someone new to Calvino, but for one who has read him before and enjoyed him, this is certainly worth the time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18126893085713433895" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Truly brilliant, The Baron in the Trees is a guiding light for all writers and readers looking to understand how to explain serious ideas with a light touch and fresh style.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_22135271" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/22135271" />
    <a title="Andrew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/220624-andrew"><img alt="Andrew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1327100694p2/220624.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/22135271?book_show_action=true">May 13, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew" href="/user/show/220624-andrew">Andrew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer22135271" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17920572526697724895">An all around story-teller in the strictest sense, Calvino guides the reader through a delightfully conceived but ultimately unfulfilling novella in The Baron in the Trees. This story is about the Baron Cosimo from a small provincial town in Italy who at the outset of teenagedom becomes fed up with the stale life of his family and the constant nagging of his father and, feeling the need to define himself amongst these trivialities, climbs into a tree and vows never to return to solid ground.<br/><br/>What</span>
  <span id="freeText17920572526697724895" style="display:none">An all around story-teller in the strictest sense, Calvino guides the reader through a delightfully conceived but ultimately unfulfilling novella in The Baron in the Trees. This story is about the Baron Cosimo from a small provincial town in Italy who at the outset of teenagedom becomes fed up with the stale life of his family and the constant nagging of his father and, feeling the need to define himself amongst these trivialities, climbs into a tree and vows never to return to solid ground.<br /><br />What follows is a traditional 18th century tale of gallantry, early mechanical innovation, enlightenment, thievery, and of course, heavy bouts of romance. Calvino deserves credit for his efforts to maintain a duality within the main character that mirrors the duality in the story itself. This point may go unnoticed because Cosimo's life in the trees does not appear as particularly strange, at least from the narrator's (Cosimo's younger brother) point of view. Cosimo at once assumes the perfunctory duties of any royal Baron, but also takes on roles as a master outdoorsman, a brigand, and a wanderer. He is both the royalty and the commoner. Similarly, the story itself is at once about a man who lives in the trees and also about the normalcy attained in that fundamentally unorthodox lifestyle. <br /><br />Calvino is essentially setting up a fantastical idea of the tree man and then killing off the fantasy with his attempt to make the man as normal as possible. To this end, the book ends up somewhat flat. To the reader of other Calvino works, this duality in character should not come as a full surprise, but the dynamic does not work as well when the the character possesses no magical or spectacular quality. <br /><br />Finally, the evident lack of Calvino's capable prose is another difficult point to ignore. At 230 pages, the reader only gets four or five paragraphs of deliciously written Calvion language, the best one coming late in the book describing the nature of sound in the woods. Passages like these, so full of considered and encapsulating words, need to happen more frequently in a story of this type from a writer of this stature.<br /><br />Despite these failures in the technical and conceptual arenas, the book communicates a somewhat stereotypical, but nonetheless important message: believe in yourself and others will too. Early on Cosimo experiences self doubt in his arboreal defiance, but remains true to his convictions, ultimately bringing him much respect and renown from both his villagers and foreign dignitaries. The moral is ideal in form: universal, vague, and readily apparent.<br /><br />The story ends with the death (technically the disappearance) of Cosimo. After fighting a losing battle against illness, Cosimo departs the scene in a most novel fashion. Without giving away the ending, the story finishes with a graceful return to nature, forcing the reader to ask: if heaven is above and hell is below, why wouldn't we all want to be a little closer to the sky? </span>
  <a data-text-id="17920572526697724895" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('6n+jfhSGwnUi38NODAZwTVk0l7U5Cdwfe41g7JlbkljQWcqsr9gLHYX0dy/783qSj2twffeEUngg8yZdVBhgwg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('gIYrBNQ2RYb8s5Ye6eYpUlFowaBx71wMXoX9QSXDiCe6oELWb2iM7luYIn8eEyONhzcmaL9i0msF+7vw6IB6vQ==')})
  }

Here are Zeno’s interminable attempts to quit smoking, his courtship of the beautiful yet unresponsive Ada, his unexpected–and unexpectedly happy–marriage to Ada’s homely sister Augusta, and his affair with a shrill-voiced aspiring singer. It is a deceptively simple story – five characters, the events of a few days, the intrigues of families and lovers.\n\nThe place is Rome. The central figure is Michele, a young man in confused but furious rebellion against the emptiness of bourgeois life. His father is dead; his mother, Mariagrazia, desperately clings to her bored lover, Leo; his sister has no hope of marriage or career and bleakly prepares to give herself to Leo as well. A frequent visitor is Leo\'s former lover, Lisa, ostensibly Mariagrazia\'s friend, a woman who feels she is in the final late bloom before age destroys beauty. She longs to make Michele her lover, but he is bored and disgusted by her pretenses, her vanity, her desperation.\n\nAll five are cast loose on the sea of modern life – obsessed with what they want, what they feel they are owed, the wrongs that have been done them, their loneliness. What Moravia destroys forever in this pitiless novel is the illusion that a world of ever-growing material comfort can ever feed the human soul.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"905855527799537384\" Un Dostoevskij lirico, ispirato, comincia a riflettere sulle disillusioni dell’esistenza e dell’amore nell’ultima opera pubblicata prima dell’arresto e della deportazione, esperienze che modificheranno in maniera radicale e definitiva la sua concezione dell’uomo e dell’arte. In questa edizione, al celebre racconto viene affiancata la visione “diurna” di Pietroburgo contenuta nei feuilletons che compongono la Cronaca di Pietroburgo, vero e proprio laboratorio per la scrittura dostoevskiana. Lo stretto legame tra pubblicistica e letteratura, che accompagnerà Dostoevskij negli anni della maturità, viene così a manifestarsi fin quasi dal suo esordio. Il racconto Le notti bianche ha ispirato il film omonimo di Luchino Visconti (1957), con Marcello Mastroianni e Maria Schell, e il film Quattro notti di un sognatore di Robert Bresson <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer712486453217277568">Italo Calvino was born in Cuba and grew up in Italy. He was a journalist and writer of short stories and novels. His best known works include the <i>Our Ancestors</i> trilogy (1952-1959), the <i>Cosmicomics</i> collection of short stories (1965), and the novels <i>Invisible Cities</i> (1972) and <i>If On a Winter's Night a Traveler</i> (1979).<br/><br/>His style is not easy to classify; much of his writing has an air reminiscent to th</span>
  <span id="freeText712486453217277568" style="display:none">Italo Calvino was born in Cuba and grew up in Italy. He was a journalist and writer of short stories and novels. His best known works include the <i>Our Ancestors</i> trilogy (1952-1959), the <i>Cosmicomics</i> collection of short stories (1965), and the novels <i>Invisible Cities</i> (1972) and <i>If On a Winter's Night a Traveler</i> (1979).<br /><br />His style is not easy to classify; much of his writing has an air reminiscent to that of fantastical fairy tales (<i>Our Ancestors, Cosmicomics</i>), although sometimes his writing is more "realistic" and in the scenic mode of observation (<i>Difficult Loves</i>, for example). Some of his writing has been called <i>postmodern</i>, reflecting on literature and the act of reading, while some has been labeled magical realist, others fables, others simply "modern". He wrote: "<i>My working method has more often than not involved the subtraction of weight. I have tried to remove weight, sometimes from people, sometimes from heavenly bodies, sometimes from cities; above all I have tried to remove weight from the structure of stories and from language.</i>"<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="712486453217277568" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
